do_single_rollout

Problem: Let $0 \le a,$ $b,$ $c \le 1.$ Find the maximum value of \[\sqrt{abc} + \sqrt{(1 - a)(1 - b)(1 - c)}.\] Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: 1 \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Since $0 \le c \le 1,$ $\sqrt{c} \le 1$ and $\sqrt{1 - c} \le 1,$ so \[\sqrt{abc} + \sqrt{(1 - a)(1 - b)(1 - c)} \le \sqrt{ab} + \sqrt{(1 - a)(1 - b)}.\]Then by AM-GM, \[\sqrt{ab} \le \frac{a + b}{2}\]and \[\sqrt{(1 - a)(1 - b)} \le \frac{(1 - a) + (1 - b)}{2} = \frac{2 - a - b}{2},\]so \[\sqrt{ab} + \sqrt{(1 - a)(1 - b)} \le \frac{a + b}{2} + \frac{2 - a - b}{2} = 1.\]Equality occurs when $a = b = c = 0,$ so the maximum value is $\boxed{1}.$

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✓, Reward: 1.00

do_single_rollout

Problem: The graph of \[4x^2 - 6x + 2 = y^2 - 10y\]is a hyperbola. Find the coordinates of its center. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: (3, 5) \boxed{(3, 5)}

Reference Answer: To get the hyperbola in standard form, we complete the square in both variables: \[\begin{aligned} 4(x^2-\tfrac32 x) + 2& = y^2-10y \\ 4(x^2-\tfrac32x+\tfrac9{16})+2+25&=(y^2-10y+25)+\tfrac94 \\ 4\left(x-\tfrac34\right)^2 + 27 &= (y-5)^2 + \tfrac94 \\\tfrac{99}{4} &= (y-5)^2 - 4\left(x-\tfrac{3}{4}\right)^2 \\ 1 &= \frac{(y-5)^2}{99/4} - \frac{\left(x-\tfrac34\right)^2}{99/16}\end{aligned}\]It follows that the center of the hyperbola is $\boxed{\left(\frac34,5\right)}.$[asy] void axes(real x0, real x1, real y0, real y1) { draw((x0,0)--(x1,0),EndArrow); draw((0,y0)--(0,y1),EndArrow); label("$x$",(x1,0),E); label("$y$",(0,y1),N); for (int i=floor(x0)+1; i<x1; ++i) draw((i,.1)--(i,-.1)); for (int i=floor(y0)+1; i<y1; ++i) draw((.1,i)--(-.1,i)); } path[] yh(real a, real b, real h, real k, real x0, real x1, bool upper=true, bool lower=true, pen color=black) { real f(real x) { return k + a / b * sqrt(b^2 + (x-h)^2); } real g(real x) { return k - a / b * sqrt(b^2 + (x-h)^2); } if (upper) { draw(graph(f, x0, x1),color, Arrows); } if (lower) { draw(graph(g, x0, x1),color, Arrows); } path [] arr = {graph(f, x0, x1), graph(g, x0, x1)}; return arr; } void xh(real a, real b, real h, real k, real y0, real y1, bool right=true, bool left=true, pen color=black) { path [] arr = yh(a, b, k, h, y0, y1, false, false); if (right) draw(reflect((0,0),(1,1))*arr[0],color, Arrows); if (left) draw(reflect((0,0),(1,1))*arr[1],color, Arrows); } void e(real a, real b, real h, real k) { draw(shift((h,k))*scale(a,b)*unitcircle); } size(8cm); axes(-5,7,-6,16); yh(sqrt(99/4),sqrt(99/16),3/4,5,-3.5,5); dot((3/4,5)); [/asy]

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

do_single_rollout

Problem: The graph of \[4x^2 - 6x + 2 = y^2 - 10y\]is a hyperbola. Find the coordinates of its center. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: (3, 5) \boxed{(3, 5)}

Reference Answer: To get the hyperbola in standard form, we complete the square in both variables: \[\begin{aligned} 4(x^2-\tfrac32 x) + 2& = y^2-10y \\ 4(x^2-\tfrac32x+\tfrac9{16})+2+25&=(y^2-10y+25)+\tfrac94 \\ 4\left(x-\tfrac34\right)^2 + 27 &= (y-5)^2 + \tfrac94 \\\tfrac{99}{4} &= (y-5)^2 - 4\left(x-\tfrac{3}{4}\right)^2 \\ 1 &= \frac{(y-5)^2}{99/4} - \frac{\left(x-\tfrac34\right)^2}{99/16}\end{aligned}\]It follows that the center of the hyperbola is $\boxed{\left(\frac34,5\right)}.$[asy] void axes(real x0, real x1, real y0, real y1) { draw((x0,0)--(x1,0),EndArrow); draw((0,y0)--(0,y1),EndArrow); label("$x$",(x1,0),E); label("$y$",(0,y1),N); for (int i=floor(x0)+1; i<x1; ++i) draw((i,.1)--(i,-.1)); for (int i=floor(y0)+1; i<y1; ++i) draw((.1,i)--(-.1,i)); } path[] yh(real a, real b, real h, real k, real x0, real x1, bool upper=true, bool lower=true, pen color=black) { real f(real x) { return k + a / b * sqrt(b^2 + (x-h)^2); } real g(real x) { return k - a / b * sqrt(b^2 + (x-h)^2); } if (upper) { draw(graph(f, x0, x1),color, Arrows); } if (lower) { draw(graph(g, x0, x1),color, Arrows); } path [] arr = {graph(f, x0, x1), graph(g, x0, x1)}; return arr; } void xh(real a, real b, real h, real k, real y0, real y1, bool right=true, bool left=true, pen color=black) { path [] arr = yh(a, b, k, h, y0, y1, false, false); if (right) draw(reflect((0,0),(1,1))*arr[0],color, Arrows); if (left) draw(reflect((0,0),(1,1))*arr[1],color, Arrows); } void e(real a, real b, real h, real k) { draw(shift((h,k))*scale(a,b)*unitcircle); } size(8cm); axes(-5,7,-6,16); yh(sqrt(99/4),sqrt(99/16),3/4,5,-3.5,5); dot((3/4,5)); [/asy]

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: Given an infinitely differentiable function \( f: \mathbb{R} \to \mathbb{R} \) such that \( f(0) = 0 \), \( f(x) > 0 \) for all \( x \neq 0 \), and \( f^{(n)}(0) = 0 \) for all \( n \in \mathbb{N} \), determine if the function \( g(x) := \sqrt{f(x)} \) is infinitely differentiable at \( x = 0 \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Since \( f(x) \) decays faster than any polynomial, \( \sqrt{f(x)} \) decays faster than any polynomial, leading to \( g'(0) = 0 \). 3. **Higher-order derivatives of \( g(x) \)**: For \( x \neq 0 \), the derivatives of \( g \) involve terms like \( f(x)^{-1/2} f^{(k)}(x) \). Given that both \( f(x) \) and its derivatives decay faster than any polynomial, these terms tend to zero as \( x \to 0 \). This implies that all higher-order derivatives of \( g \) at zero exist and are zero. 4. **Inductive argument**: Using the fact that \( f(x) = (g(x))^2 \) and all derivatives of \( f \) at zero are zero, we can inductively show that all derivatives of \( g \) at zero must be zero. This is because each higher-order derivative of \( f \) involves products of derivatives of \( g \), which are forced to zero by the given conditions on \( f \). 5. **Flatness of \( g(x) \)**: Since \( f(x) \) is flat at zero, \( \sqrt{f(x)} \) is also flat at zero. This means \( \sqrt{f(x)} \) decays faster than any polynomial, implying all its derivatives at zero are zero. Therefore, the function \( g(x) = \sqrt{f(x)} \) is infinitely differentiable at \( x = 0 \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Therefore, putting it all together, \( g(x) \) is infinitely differentiable at zero. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> Given an infinitely differentiable function \( f: \mathbb{R} \to \mathbb{R} \) such that \( f(0) = 0 \), \( f(x) > 0 \) for all \( x \neq 0 \), and \( f^{(n)}(0) = 0 \) for all \( n \in \mathbb{N} \), we need to determine if the function \( g(x) := \sqrt{f(x)} \) is infinitely differentiable at \( x = 0 \). 1. **Behavior of \( f(x) \) near zero**: Since all derivatives of \( f \) at zero are zero, \( f(x) \) approaches zero faster than any polynomial as \( x \to 0 \). This means \( f(x) \) is a flat function at the origin. 2. **First derivative of \( g(x) \)**: The first derivative of \( g \) at zero is the limit as \( x \to 0 \) of \( \sqrt{f(x)} / x \). Since \( f(x) \) decays faster than any polynomial, \( \sqrt{f(x)} \) decays faster than any polynomial, leading to \( g'(0) = 0 \). 3. **Higher-order derivatives of \( g(x) \)**: For \( x \neq 0 \), the derivatives of \( g \) involve terms like \( f(x)^{-1/2} f^{(k)}(x) \). Given that both \( f(x) \) and its derivatives decay faster than any polynomial, these terms tend to zero as \( x \to 0 \). This implies that all higher-order derivatives of \( g \) at zero exist and are zero. 4. **Inductive argument**: Using the fact that \( f(x) = (g(x))^2 \) and all derivatives of \( f \) at zero are zero, we can inductively show that all derivatives of \( g \) at zero must be zero. This is because each higher-order derivative of \( f \) involves products of derivatives of \( g \), which are forced to zero by the given conditions on \( f \). 5. **Flatness of \( g(x) \)**: Since \( f(x) \) is flat at zero, \( \sqrt{f(x)} \) is also flat at zero. This means \( \sqrt{f(x)} \) decays faster than any polynomial, implying all its derivatives at zero are zero. Therefore, the function \( g(x) = \sqrt{f(x)} \) is infinitely differentiable at \( x = 0 \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

So, if \( f(x) \) is flat at zero, and \( f(x) \geq 0 \), then \( g(x) = \sqrt{f(x)} \) is also flat at zero, hence smooth. But how to show that \( g(x) \) is flat at zero? Well, suppose we want to show that all derivatives of \( g(x) \) at zero are zero. Let's proceed by induction. Base case: \( g(0) = 0 \), which is given. Inductive step: Suppose that all derivatives of \( g \) at zero up to order \( n \) are zero. We need to show that \( g^{(n+1)}(0) = 0 \). To compute \( g^{(n+1)}(0) \), we can consider the limit of \( g^{(n)}(x) / x \) as \( x \to 0 \) (if \( n = 0 \), it's the first derivative). But since \( g^{(n)}(0) = 0 \), the derivative from the definition is the limit as \( x \to 0 \) of \( g^{(n)}(x) / x \). However, to use the inductive hypothesis, we might need a better approach. Perhaps using the Faà di Bruno's formula or some recursive relation. Alternatively, consider the Taylor expansion of \( g(x) \) around zero. Since all derivatives of \( f(x) \) at zero are zero, the formal Taylor series of \( f(x) \) is zero. However, \( g(x) = \sqrt{f(x)} \), which would also have a zero Taylor series. But even though the Taylor series of \( g(x) \) is zero, \( g(x) \) might still be smooth if it is flat at zero. Alternatively, we can use the Borel's theorem, which says that for any sequence of real numbers, there exists a smooth function with those numbers as derivatives at a point. But I don't think that's helpful here. Wait, maybe instead, use the fact that \( f(x) \) is flat at zero, so for any \( n \in \mathbb{N} \), \( f(x) = o(x^n) \) as \( x \to 0 \). Then, \( \sqrt{f(x)} = o(x^{n/2}) \). Therefore, for any \( m \in \mathbb{N} \), taking \( n = 2m \), we have \( \sqrt{f(x)} = o(x^m) \), so \( \sqrt{f(x)} \) is also flat at zero. Therefore, \( g(x) = \sqrt{f(x)} \) is flat at zero, which would imply that all its derivatives at zero are zero. Hence, \( g(x) \) is smooth at zero. But wait, does \( \sqrt{f(x)} = o(x^m) \) for any \( m \) imply that all derivatives of \( g \) at zero are zero? Yes, actually. Because if a function \( h(x) \) satisfies \( h(x) = o(x^m) \) as \( x \to 0 \) for every \( m \), then all its derivatives at zero must be zero. Here's why: suppose \( h \) is differentiable at zero, then \( h'(0) = \lim_{x \to 0} h(x)/x \). But \( h(x) = o(x^1) \), so \( h(x)/x \to 0 \), hence \( h'(0) = 0 \). Similarly, if \( h'(x) \) exists near zero and \( h'(x) = o(x^{m}) \) for every \( m \), then \( h''(0) = 0 \), and so on. So, by induction, if \( h(x) \) is smooth and \( h(x) = o(x^m) \) for every \( m \), then all derivatives at zero are zero. Therefore, \( h \) is flat at zero. Therefore, since \( f(x) = o(x^n) \) for every \( n \), \( \sqrt{f(x)} = o(x^{n/2}) \). For any fixed \( m \), taking \( n = 2m \), we have \( \sqrt{f(x)} = o(x^{m}) \). Hence, \( \sqrt{f(x)} \) is flat at zero, which implies all its derivatives at zero are zero, so \( g(x) \) is infinitely differentiable at zero with all derivatives zero. Therefore, the answer should be yes, \( g(x) \) is infinitely differentiable at \( x = 0 \). But let me verify this logic once more. The key point is that if \( f(x) \) is flat at zero, then \( \sqrt{f(x)} \) is also flat at zero. Flatness here means decaying faster than any positive power of \( x \), which is preserved under square roots. Because if \( f(x) \leq C x^n \) for any \( n \), then \( \sqrt{f(x)} \leq \sqrt{C} x^{n/2} \), which can be made smaller than any \( x^m \) by choosing \( n = 2m \). Therefore, \( \sqrt{f(x)} \) is also flat. Then, the result follows from Borel's theorem or the fact that flat functions have all derivatives zero. Alternatively, since \( g(x) \) is smooth everywhere except possibly at zero, and near zero, it's flat, which would imply smoothness at zero. Because if a function is smooth on \( \mathbb{R} \setminus \{0\} \) and flat at zero, then it's smooth everywhere. This is because the flatness at zero allows all derivatives to be zero there, and the derivatives from the right and left (which is only one side in this case) both match at zero. Therefore, putting it all together, \( g(x) \) is infinitely differentiable at zero. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> Given an infinitely differentiable function \( f: \mathbb{R} \to \mathbb{R} \) such that \( f(0) = 0 \), \( f(x) > 0 \) for all \( x \neq 0 \), and \( f^{(n)}(0) = 0 \) for all \( n \in \mathbb{N} \), we need to determine if the function \( g(x) := \sqrt{f(x)} \) is infinitely differentiable at \( x = 0 \). 1. **Behavior of \( f(x) \) near zero**: Since all derivatives of \( f \) at zero are zero, \( f(x) \) approaches zero faster than any polynomial as \( x \to 0 \). This means \( f(x) \) is a flat function at the origin. 2. **First derivative of \( g(x) \)**: The first derivative of \( g \) at zero is the limit as \( x \to 0 \) of \( \sqrt{f(x)} / x \). Since \( f(x) \) decays faster than any polynomial, \( \sqrt{f(x)} \) decays faster than any polynomial, leading to \( g'(0) = 0 \). 3. **Higher-order derivatives of \( g(x) \)**: For \( x \neq 0 \), the derivatives of \( g \) involve terms like \( f(x)^{-1/2} f^{(k)}(x) \). Given that both \( f(x) \) and its derivatives decay faster than any polynomial, these terms tend to zero as \( x \to 0 \). This implies that all higher-order derivatives of \( g \) at zero exist and are zero. 4. **Inductive argument**: Using the fact that \( f(x) = (g(x))^2 \) and all derivatives of \( f \) at zero are zero, we can inductively show that all derivatives of \( g \) at zero must be zero. This is because each higher-order derivative of \( f \) involves products of derivatives of \( g \), which are forced to zero by the given conditions on \( f \). 5. **Flatness of \( g(x) \)**: Since \( f(x) \) is flat at zero, \( \sqrt{f(x)} \) is also flat at zero. This means \( \sqrt{f(x)} \) decays faster than any polynomial, implying all its derivatives at zero are zero. Therefore, the function \( g(x) = \sqrt{f(x)} \) is infinitely differentiable at \( x = 0 \). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: The first term of an arithmetic sequence is $2$. The sum of the third and the sixth terms is $25$. What is the fourth term? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: 6. \boxed{6}

Reference Answer: Call the difference between two consecutive terms $x$. The third term is then $2+2x$, and the sixth term is $2+5x$. So, $25 = (2+2x) + (2+5x)$. Solving for $x$ gives $7x = 21$, or $x = 3$. So, the fourth term is $2 + 3\cdot 3 = \boxed{11}$.
